Good! I can see that the stream's `recvLoop` DOES handle cleaning up pending requests in the defer block (lines 364-366). So the stream itself handles cleanup properly.

But wait, let me re-analyze the issue. Let me look at what happens in this scenario more carefully:

1. Requests are collected in `tsoBatchController`
2. `processRequests` is called successfully (returns nil)
3. The requests are sent to the stream's `pendingRequests` channel
4. The dispatcher sets `tsoBatchController = nil` and starts collecting the next batch
5. Before the stream's `recvLoop` can process the response, an error occurs on the stream
6. The dispatcher detects the error at line 263 via `stream.GetRecvError()`
7. The dispatcher calls `cancelCollectedRequests` with the NEW batch controller

The question is: are the requests that were sent to the stream's `pendingRequests` channel properly cancelled?

Let me check the timing more carefully. Looking at line 263:
```go
if err = stream.GetRecvError(); err != nil {
```

This checks if the stream has encountered an error. If it has, the stream's `recvLoop` has already exited and cleaned up all pending requests in the `pendingRequests` channel. So that should be fine.

But wait! Let me look at the scenario more carefully. What if:
1. `processRequests` is called and sends the request successfully
2. The stream is working fine, no error yet
3. The dispatcher continues and starts collecting the next batch
4. THEN an error occurs on the stream
5. The dispatcher checks for errors at line 263 with the NEW batch, not the old one

Ah, I see the issue now! Let me trace through the code again more carefully:

Looking at lines 262-271:
```go
// Check if any error has occurred on this stream when receiving asynchronously.
if err = stream.GetRecvError(); err != nil {
    exit := !td.handleProcessRequestError(ctx, bo, conCtxMgr, streamURL, err)
    stream = nil
    if exit {
        td.cancelCollectedRequests(tsoBatchController, invalidStreamID, errors.WithStack(err))
        return
    }
    continue
}
```

This code is checking for errors BEFORE sending the batch. So if there's a batch that has ALREADY been sent (previous iteration), and THEN the stream encounters an error, this code path will:
1. Cancel the CURRENT batch (which hasn't been sent yet)
2. Continue the loop to try with a new stream

But the PREVIOUS batch that was already sent to the stream should be handled by the stream's cleanup logic in the defer block.

Let me look more carefully at the processRequests flow and callback mechanism:

   401	func (td *tsoDispatcher) processRequests(
   402	        stream *tsoStream, tbc *batch.Controller[*Request], done chan struct{},
   403	) error {
   404	        // `done` must be guaranteed to be eventually called.
   405	        var (
   406	                requests     = tbc.GetCollectedRequests()
   407	                traceRegions = make([]*trace.Region, 0, len(requests))
   408	                spans        = make([]opentracing.Span, 0, len(requests))
   409	        )
   410	        for _, req := range requests {
   411	                traceRegions = append(traceRegions, trace.StartRegion(req.requestCtx, "pdclient.tsoReqSend"))
   412	                if span := opentracing.SpanFromContext(req.requestCtx); span != nil && span.Tracer() != nil {
   413	                        spans = append(spans, span.Tracer().StartSpan("pdclient.processRequests", opentracing.ChildOf(span.Context())))
   414	                }
   415	        }
   416	        defer func() {
   417	                for i := len(spans) - 1; i >= 0; i-- {
   418	                        spans[i].Finish()
   419	                }
   420	                for i := len(traceRegions) - 1; i >= 0; i-- {
   421	                        traceRegions[i].End()
   422	                }
   423	        }()
   424	
   425	        var (
   426	                count              = int64(len(requests))
   427	                svcDiscovery       = td.provider.getServiceDiscovery()
   428	                clusterID          = svcDiscovery.GetClusterID()
   429	                keyspaceID         = svcDiscovery.GetKeyspaceID()
   430	                reqKeyspaceGroupID = svcDiscovery.GetKeyspaceGroupID()
   431	        )
   432	
   433	        // Load latest allocated ts for monotonicity assertion.
   434	        tsoInfoBeforeReq := td.latestTSOInfo.Load()
   435	
   436	        cb := func(result tsoRequestResult, reqKeyspaceGroupID uint32, err error) {
   437	                // As golang doesn't allow double-closing a channel, here is implicitly a check that the callback
   438	                // is never called twice or called while it's also being cancelled elsewhere.
   439	                close(done)
   440	
   441	                defer td.batchBufferPool.Put(tbc)
   442	                if err != nil {
   443	                        td.cancelCollectedRequests(tbc, stream.streamID, err)
   444	                        return
   445	                }
   446	
   447	                curTSOInfo := &tsoInfo{
   448	                        tsoServer:           stream.getServerURL(),
   449	                        reqKeyspaceGroupID:  reqKeyspaceGroupID,
   450	                        respKeyspaceGroupID: result.respKeyspaceGroupID,
   451	                        respReceivedAt:      time.Now(),
   452	                        physical:            result.physical,
   453	                        logical:             result.logical,
   454	                        sourceStreamID:      stream.streamID,
   455	                }
   456	                // `logical` is the largest ts's logical part here, we need to do the subtracting before we finish each TSO request.
   457	                firstLogical := result.logical - int64(result.count) + 1
   458	                // Do the check before releasing the token.
   459	                td.checkMonotonicity(tsoInfoBeforeReq, curTSOInfo, firstLogical)
   460	                td.doneCollectedRequests(tbc, result.physical, firstLogical, stream.streamID)
   461	        }
   462	
   463	        err := stream.processRequests(
   464	                clusterID, keyspaceID, reqKeyspaceGroupID,
   465	                count, tbc.GetExtraBatchingStartTime(), cb)
   466	        if err != nil {
   467	                close(done)
   468	
   469	                td.cancelCollectedRequests(tbc, stream.streamID, err)
   470	                return err
   471	        }
   472	        return nil
   473	}

    17	import (
    18	        "context"

    20	
    21	        "go.uber.org/zap"

    23	        "github.com/pingcap/log"

    26	)
    27	
    28	// The `cancel` function will be invoked once the specified `timeout` elapses without receiving a `done` signal.
    29	type deadline struct {
    30	        timer  *time.Timer
    31	        done   chan struct{}
    32	        cancel context.CancelFunc
    33	}
    34	
    35	// Watcher is used to watch and manage the deadlines.
    36	type Watcher struct {
    37	        ctx    context.Context
    38	        source string
    39	        Ch     chan *deadline
    40	}
    41	
    42	// NewWatcher is used to create a new deadline watcher.
    43	func NewWatcher(ctx context.Context, capacity int, source string) *Watcher {
    44	        watcher := &Watcher{
    45	                ctx:    ctx,
    46	                source: source,
    47	                Ch:     make(chan *deadline, capacity),
    48	        }
    49	        go watcher.Watch()
    50	        return watcher
    51	}
    52	
    53	// Watch is used to watch the deadlines and invoke the `cancel` function when the deadline is reached.
    54	// The `err` will be returned if the deadline is reached.
    55	func (w *Watcher) Watch() {
    56	        log.Info("[pd] start the deadline watcher", zap.String("source", w.source))
    57	        defer log.Info("[pd] exit the deadline watcher", zap.String("source", w.source))
    58	        for {
    59	                select {
    60	                case d := <-w.Ch:
    61	                        select {
    62	                        case <-d.timer.C:
    63	                                log.Error("[pd] the deadline is reached", zap.String("source", w.source))
    64	                                d.cancel()
    65	                                timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Put(d.timer)
    66	                        case <-d.done:
    67	                                timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Put(d.timer)
    68	                        case <-w.ctx.Done():
    69	                                timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Put(d.timer)
    70	                                return
    71	                        }
    72	                case <-w.ctx.Done():
    73	                        return
    74	                }
    75	        }
    76	}
    77	
    78	// Start is used to start a deadline. It returns a channel which will be closed when the deadline is reached.
    79	// Returns nil if the deadline is not started.
    80	func (w *Watcher) Start(
    81	        ctx context.Context,
    82	        timeout time.Duration,
    83	        cancel context.CancelFunc,
    84	) chan struct{} {
    85	        // Check if the watcher is already canceled.
    86	        select {
    87	        case <-w.ctx.Done():
    88	                return nil
    89	        case <-ctx.Done():
    90	                return nil
    91	        default:
    92	        }
    93	        // Initialize the deadline.
    94	        timer := timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Get(timeout)
    95	        d := &deadline{
    96	                timer:  timer,
    97	                done:   make(chan struct{}),
    98	                cancel: cancel,
    99	        }
   100	        // Send the deadline to the watcher.
   101	        select {
   102	        case <-w.ctx.Done():
   103	                timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Put(timer)
   104	                return nil
   105	        case <-ctx.Done():
   106	                timerutil.GlobalTimerPool.Put(timer)
   107	                return nil
   108	        case w.Ch <- d:
   109	                return d.done
   110	        }
   111	}
   112

5. Since the request was already added to the channel in step 1, `hasReq` will be true
6. The code breaks out of the loop at line 429
7. In the defer block:
   - Line 339-342: If `hasReq` is true, it calls the callback with the error
   - Line 364-366: It drains all remaining requests from `s.pendingRequests`

Wait, this should work correctly. The callback will be called in line 341 if hasReq is true, which will close the `done` channel and release the deadline watcher goroutine.

Let me look for another scenario. What if the stream is cancelled BEFORE receiving?

Actually, I think I found the issue! Look at line 408:
```go
case currentReq = <-s.pendingRequests:
```

This is a non-blocking select (there's a default case). If `Recv()` returns an error (line 403), it TRIES to pop from `pendingRequests`, but if nothing is there yet (race condition), it sets `hasReq = false`.

But wait, the defer block at line 364-366 drains ALL remaining requests from `pendingRequests`, so they should still be handled...

Let me think about this differently. Let me look at what happens when an error is detected in the dispatcher BEFORE calling processRequests. Looking at lines 262-271 again:

```go
// Check if any error has occurred on this stream when receiving asynchronously.
if err = stream.GetRecvError(); err != nil {
    exit := !td.handleProcessRequestError(ctx, bo, conCtxMgr, streamURL, err)
    stream = nil
    if exit {
        td.cancelCollectedRequests(tsoBatchController, invalidStreamID, errors.WithStack(err))
        return
    }
    continue
}
```

This check happens BEFORE sending the batch. So if a stream error is detected here, the current batch in `tsoBatchController` is cancelled. But what about batches that were ALREADY sent in previous iterations?

Ah! I think I found the real issue. Let me trace through this scenario carefully:

1. Iteration 1: Batch A is collected, stream is good, `processRequests` succeeds, `tsoBatchController = nil`, batch A is now waiting asynchronously
2. Iteration 2: New batch B is collected in a new `tsoBatchController`
3. The stream encounters an error AFTER batch A was sent but BEFORE batch B is sent
4. The error is detected at line 263
5. Line 264-270: The code calls `handleProcessRequestError` and sets `stream = nil`, then continues the loop
6. Line 270: `continue` - goes back to the top of the loop
7. The code DOES NOT cancel batch B! It just continues and will try to send it again with a new stream

Wait, that's not right either. Let me re-read...

Actually, looking at line 267:
```go
td.cancelCollectedRequests(tsoBatchController, invalidStreamID, errors.WithStack(err))
```

This DOES cancel the current batch (batch B). But batch A, which was already sent and is waiting for a response, should be handled by the stream's cleanup in the defer block.

Let me look more carefully at the cleanup in the stream's defer block:

```go
close(s.pendingRequests)

// Cancel remaining pending requests.
for req := range s.pendingRequests {
    req.callback(tsoRequestResult{}, req.reqKeyspaceGroupID, errors.WithStack(finishWithErr))
}
```

This drains all requests from `pendingRequests`. So any request that was sent via `processRequests` and added to `pendingRequests` should be handled here.

   271	        clusterID uint64, keyspaceID, keyspaceGroupID uint32, count int64, batchStartTime time.Time, callback onFinishedCallback,
   272	) error {
   273	        start := time.Now()
   274	
   275	        // Check if the stream is closing or closed, in which case no more requests should be put in.
   276	        // Note that the prevState should be restored very soon, as the receiver may check
   277	        prevState := s.state.Swap(streamStateSending)
   278	        switch prevState {
   279	        case streamStateIdle:
   280	                // Expected case
   281	        case streamStateClosing:
   282	                s.state.Store(prevState)
   283	                err := s.GetRecvError()

   287	                }

   291	        default:

Look at lines 308-316. When `s.stream.Send()` fails, the code:
1. Logs a warning
2. Returns `nil` (not an error!)

The comment at lines 309-314 explains that the request was already put into `pendingRequests`, so it will be handled by the recvLoop.

BUT there's a race condition here! Here's the problematic scenario:

1. `processRequests` is called, and the request is added to `pendingRequests` channel (line 296)
2. `s.stream.Send()` is called and FAILS (line 308)
3. The function returns `nil` (line 316), so the dispatcher thinks the request was sent successfully
4. The dispatcher's `processRequests` function returns `nil` (line 472), so `tsoBatchController = nil` (line 344)
5. The recvLoop is blocked on `s.stream.Recv()` (line 403) and hasn't yet detected the error
6. The dispatcher continues and collects the next batch
7. The dispatcher checks for errors with `stream.GetRecvError()` (line 263), which returns nil because the recvLoop hasn't detected the error yet
8. The dispatcher sends the next batch
9. NOW the recvLoop detects the error on `Recv()` and breaks out of the loop
10. In the defer block, it processes the `currentReq` that it tried to receive (the SECOND batch)
11. Then it drains `pendingRequests` - but wait! The FIRST batch's request is still in `pendingRequests` because `Send()` failed, not `Recv()`!

Actually, wait. Let me re-read the recvLoop logic more carefully...

Looking at lines 407-412:
```go
select {
case currentReq = <-s.pendingRequests:
    hasReq = true
default:
    hasReq = false
}
```

This is non-blocking. If a request was added to `pendingRequests` (even if `Send()` failed), it will be popped here. Then:
- If `Recv()` returned an error (line 417), the code breaks the loop
- In the defer block (line 339-342), if `hasReq` is true, it calls the callback with the error
- Then it drains remaining requests from `pendingRequests` (line 364-366)

So this should work correctly...

Let me think about a different scenario. What if there are MULTIPLE requests in `pendingRequests`?

1. Batch A is sent, added to `pendingRequests`
2. Batch B is sent, added to `pendingRequests`
3. The recvLoop calls `Recv()` and gets an error
4. It pops ONE request from `pendingRequests` (Batch A) at line 408
5. It sets `hasReq = true` and breaks the loop
6. In the defer block, it handles Batch A (line 341)
7. Then it drains remaining requests (Batch B) from `pendingRequests` (line 364-366)

This should work correctly too!

    15	package connectionctx
    16	
    17	import (
    18	        "context"
    19	        "math/rand"
    20	        "sync"
    21	)
    22	
    23	// ConnectionCtx wraps the stream connection with its context info.
    24	type ConnectionCtx[T any] struct {
    25	        Ctx    context.Context
    26	        Cancel context.CancelFunc
    27	        // Current URL of the stream connection.
    28	        StreamURL string
    29	        // Current stream to send the gRPC requests.
    30	        Stream T
    31	}
    32	
    33	// Manager is used to manage the connection contexts.
    34	type Manager[T any] struct {
    35	        sync.RWMutex
    36	        connectionCtxs map[string]*ConnectionCtx[T]
    37	}
    38	
    39	// NewManager is used to create a new connection context manager.
    40	func NewManager[T any]() *Manager[T] {
    41	        return &Manager[T]{
    42	                connectionCtxs: make(map[string]*ConnectionCtx[T], 3),
    43	        }
    44	}
    45	
    46	// Exist is used to check if the connection context exists by the given URL.
    47	func (c *Manager[T]) Exist(url string) bool {
    48	        c.RLock()
    49	        defer c.RUnlock()
    50	        _, ok := c.connectionCtxs[url]
    51	        return ok
    52	}
    53	
    54	// Store is used to store the connection context, `overwrite` is used to force the store operation
    55	// no matter whether the connection context exists before, which is false by default.
    56	func (c *Manager[T]) Store(ctx context.Context, cancel context.CancelFunc, url string, stream T, overwrite ...bool) {
    57	        c.Lock()
    58	        defer c.Unlock()
    59	        overwriteFlag := false
    60	        if len(overwrite) > 0 {
    61	                overwriteFlag = overwrite[0]
    62	        }
    63	        _, ok := c.connectionCtxs[url]
    64	        if !overwriteFlag && ok {
    65	                return
    66	        }
    67	        c.storeLocked(ctx, cancel, url, stream)
    68	}
    69	
    70	func (c *Manager[T]) storeLocked(ctx context.Context, cancel context.CancelFunc, url string, stream T) {
    71	        c.releaseLocked(url)
    72	        c.connectionCtxs[url] = &ConnectionCtx[T]{ctx, cancel, url, stream}
    73	}
    74	
    75	// CleanAllAndStore is used to store the connection context exclusively. It will release
    76	// all other connection contexts. `stream` is optional, if it is not provided, all
    77	// connection contexts other than the given `url` will be released.
    78	func (c *Manager[T]) CleanAllAndStore(ctx context.Context, cancel context.CancelFunc, url string, stream ...T) {
    79	        c.Lock()
    80	        defer c.Unlock()
    81	        // Remove all other `connectionCtx`s.
    82	        c.gcLocked(func(curURL string) bool {
    83	                return curURL != url
    84	        })
    85	        if len(stream) == 0 {
    86	                return
    87	        }
    88	        c.storeLocked(ctx, cancel, url, stream[0])
    89	}
    90	
    91	// GC is used to release all connection contexts that match the given condition.
    92	func (c *Manager[T]) GC(condition func(url string) bool) {
    93	        c.Lock()
    94	        defer c.Unlock()
    95	        c.gcLocked(condition)
    96	}
    97	
    98	func (c *Manager[T]) gcLocked(condition func(url string) bool) {
    99	        for url := range c.connectionCtxs {
   100	                if condition(url) {
   101	                        c.releaseLocked(url)
   102	                }
   103	        }
   104	}
   105	
   106	// ReleaseAll is used to release all connection contexts.
   107	func (c *Manager[T]) ReleaseAll() {
   108	        c.GC(func(string) bool { return true })
   109	}
   110	
   111	// Release is used to delete a connection context from the connection context map and release the resources.
   112	func (c *Manager[T]) Release(url string) {
   113	        c.Lock()
   114	        defer c.Unlock()
   115	        c.releaseLocked(url)
   116	}
   117	
   118	func (c *Manager[T]) releaseLocked(url string) {
   119	        cc, ok := c.connectionCtxs[url]
   120	        if !ok {
   121	                return
   122	        }
   123	        cc.Cancel()
   124	        delete(c.connectionCtxs, url)
   125	}
   126	
   127	// RandomlyPick picks a connection context from the connection context map randomly.
   128	// It uses the reservoir sampling algorithm to randomly pick one connection context.
   129	func (c *Manager[T]) RandomlyPick() *ConnectionCtx[T] {
   130	        c.RLock()
   131	        defer c.RUnlock()
   132	        idx := 0
   133	        var connectionCtx *ConnectionCtx[T]
   134	        for _, cc := range c.connectionCtxs {
   135	                j := rand.Intn(idx + 1)
   136	                if j < 1 {
   137	                        connectionCtx = cc
   138	                }
   139	                idx++
   140	        }
   141	        return connectionCtx
   142	}
   143	
   144	// GetConnectionCtx is used to get a connection context from the connection context map by the given URL.
   145	func (c *Manager[T]) GetConnectionCtx(url string) *ConnectionCtx[T] {
   146	        c.RLock()
   147	        defer c.RUnlock()
   148	        cc, ok := c.connectionCtxs[url]
   149	        if !ok {
   150	                return nil
   151	        }
   152	        return cc
   153	}
   154

2025-12-26 21:05:13,789 - DEBUG - swea-lm-tikv__pd-9618 - total_tokens_sent=0, total_tokens_received=3,905, total_cost=0.00, total_api_calls=23
2025-12-26 21:05:13,792 - INFO - swea-agent-tikv__pd-9618 - 💭 THOUGHT
Good! So when `Release` is called (line 112), it calls `cc.Cancel()` (line 123), which cancels the stream's context. This should cause the stream's recvLoop to exit and clean up all pending requests.

So the cleanup mechanism seems correct. Let me think about where the leak could be...

AH! I think I found it! Let me look at the sequence again more carefully:

Looking at the dispatcher's main loop, specifically at line 263:

```go
// Check if any error has occurred on this stream when receiving asynchronously.
if err = stream.GetRecvError(); err != nil {
    exit := !td.handleProcessRequestError(ctx, bo, conCtxMgr, streamURL, err)
    stream = nil
    if exit {
        td.cancelCollectedRequests(tsoBatchController, invalidStreamID, errors.WithStack(err))
        return
    }
    continue
}
```

The issue is that `stream.GetRecvError()` only returns an error if the stream's recvLoop has ALREADY exited and set the error. But what if:

1. A batch was just sent successfully via `processRequests`
2. The recvLoop is still running and hasn't encountered an error yet
3. The dispatcher collects the next batch
4. The dispatcher checks `stream.GetRecvError()` which returns `nil` because the recvLoop is still running
5. The dispatcher tries to send the next batch via `processRequests`
6. Inside `stream.processRequests`, `s.stream.Send()` fails
7. The function returns `nil` (line 316), which is wrong! It should signal the error somehow

Wait, let me re-read the comment at lines 309-314:

```go
// As the request is already put into `pendingRequests`, the request should finally be canceled by the recvLoop.
// So skip returning error here to avoid
// if err == io.EOF {
//      return errors.WithStack(errs.ErrClientTSOStreamClosed)
// }
// return errors.WithStack(err)
```

The comment says "the request should finally be canceled by the recvLoop". But if `Send()` failed, it might be because the stream is already broken. The recvLoop might be blocked on `Recv()` and won't know about the Send error.

Actually, let me check if grpc streams are bi-directional and if a Send error will cause Recv to error too...

But more importantly, I think the real issue is that when `Send()` fails, the function returns `nil`, which causes the dispatcher to think everything is fine and set `tsoBatchController = nil`. But if the recvLoop doesn't properly handle this case, the requests could be leaked!

Let me create a test to reproduce this issue:

str_replace_editor create /testbed/test_goroutine_leak.go --file_text 'package main

import (
	"context"
	"fmt"
	"runtime"
	"sync"
	"time"

	"github.com/tikv/pd/client/clients/tso"
	"github.com/tikv/pd/client/opt"
	cctx "github.com/tikv/pd/client/pkg/connectionctx"
	sd "github.com/tikv/pd/client/servicediscovery"
)

// Mock implementation for testing
type mockTSOServiceProvider struct {
	option       *opt.Option
	createStream func(ctx context.Context) *tso.tsoStream
	conCtxMgr    *cctx.Manager[*tso.tsoStream]
}

func newMockTSOServiceProvider(option *opt.Option, createStream func(ctx context.Context) *tso.tsoStream) *mockTSOServiceProvider {
	return &mockTSOServiceProvider{
		option:       option,
		createStream: createStream,
		conCtxMgr:    cctx.NewManager[*tso.tsoStream](),
	}
}

func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getOption() *opt.Option {
	return m.option
}

func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getServiceDiscovery() sd.ServiceDiscovery {
	return sd.NewMockServiceDiscovery([]string{"mock:///"}, nil)
}

func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getConnectionCtxMgr() *cctx.Manager[*tso.tsoStream] {
	return m.conCtxMgr
}

func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) updateConnectionCtxs(ctx context.Context) bool {
	if m.conCtxMgr.Exist("mock:///") {
		return true
	}
	cctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(ctx)
	var stream *tso.tsoStream
	if m.createStream != nil {
		stream = m.createStream(ctx)
	}
	if stream != nil {
		m.conCtxMgr.Store(cctx, cancel, "mock:///", stream)
		return true
	}
	cancel()
	return false
}

func main() {
	fmt.Println("Testing goroutine leak scenario...")

	// Get initial goroutine count
	runtime.GC()
	time.Sleep(100 * time.Millisecond)
	initialGoroutines := runtime.NumGoroutine()
	fmt.Printf("Initial goroutines: %d\n", initialGoroutines)

	option := opt.NewOption()
	option.Timeout = time.Hour

	ctx := context.Background()

	// We'"'"'ll test if goroutines leak when stream errors occur
	// Create a provider that will help us simulate stream errors
	provider := newMockTSOServiceProvider(option, nil)

	// Note: This is a simplified test - the actual leak reproduction requires
	// access to internal tsoDispatcher methods and stream creation
	fmt.Println("Test setup complete")

	// Check goroutine count
	runtime.GC()
	time.Sleep(100 * time.Millisecond)
	finalGoroutines := runtime.NumGoroutine()
	fmt.Printf("Final goroutines: %d\n", finalGoroutines)

	if finalGoroutines > initialGoroutines+2 {
		fmt.Printf("WARNING: Potential goroutine leak detected! Leaked: %d\n", finalGoroutines-initialGoroutines)
	} else {
		fmt.Println("No significant goroutine leak detected in this simplified test")
	}
}
'

Good! There are existing tests but no test specifically for the goroutine leak scenario described in the PR. Let me look more closely at the actual problem. Based on my analysis and the PR description, I now understand the issue better:

The bug is that when a stream error occurs, if there are pending requests that have already been sent to the stream (via `processRequests`), but the stream's recvLoop hasn't processed them yet, those requests might not be properly cancelled if the dispatcher doesn't wait for the stream cleanup to complete before releasing the stream reference.

Let me look at the exact scenario again. The key insight is in the comment on line 309-314 of `stream.go`:

```go
// As the request is already put into `pendingRequests`, the request should finally be canceled by the recvLoop.
```

This assumes that the recvLoop will properly handle the cleanup. But there's a problem: when `stream.Send()` fails (line 308), the function returns `nil` (line 316), making the dispatcher think everything is fine. However, if the Send error is due to a broken stream, the Recv loop might not properly drain the `pendingRequests` channel if it's also stuck waiting for a Recv.

Actually, upon further analysis, I think the REAL issue is simpler. Let me trace through a specific scenario:

1. The dispatcher's `processRequests` is called
2. The request is added to `s.pendingRequests` (line 296 of stream.go)
3. `s.stream.Send()` is called (line 308) and succeeds
4. The function returns `nil` (line 320)
5. The dispatcher sets `tsoBatchController = nil` (line 344 of dispatcher.go)
6. The dispatcher collects the next batch
7. The dispatcher checks for errors at line 263, and detects a stream error
8. The dispatcher calls `handleProcessRequestError` which calls `conCtxMgr.Release(streamURL)` (line 375)
9. This cancels the stream's context
10. The stream's recvLoop should exit and clean up

The issue is that there might be a race condition where the dispatcher continues before the stream has finished cleanup, and creates a NEW stream with the same URL before the old one is fully cleaned up.

But actually, looking at the tests passing, the basic cleanup seems to work. Let me look for a more subtle issue...

AH! I think I finally found it! Let me check the exact flow in the dispatcher when checking for stream errors:

Looking at lines 262-271 of dispatcher.go:

```go
// Check if any error has occurred on this stream when receiving asynchronously.
if err = stream.GetRecvError(); err != nil {
    exit := !td.handleProcessRequestError(ctx, bo, conCtxMgr, streamURL, err)
    stream = nil
    if exit {
        td.cancelCollectedRequests(tsoBatchController, invalidStreamID, errors.WithStack(err))
        return
    }
    continue
}
```

The problem is that this code:
1. Checks for errors on the stream
2. If there's an error, it releases the stream from the connection manager
3. Sets `stream = nil`
4. Cancels the CURRENT batch (if exiting)
5. Continues the loop

But it does NOT wait for any PREVIOUSLY SENT batches to be cleaned up! The batches that were already sent to the stream (in previous iterations) are still waiting for responses asynchronously. When the stream is released, its context is cancelled, which should trigger cleanup in the recvLoop. But the dispatcher doesn't wait for this cleanup to complete!

This means that:
1. The dispatcher continues and might create a new stream
2. The old stream's cleanup is running asynchronously
3. The requests from the old stream might not be properly finished before the dispatcher exits or creates a new stream

Actually, wait. The cleanup should still happen asynchronously even if the dispatcher doesn't wait. The stream's defer block should handle all pending requests.

Let me look at the actual bug more carefully. Based on the PR description: "pending requests were not being properly cancelled when stream errors occurred". This suggests that the callbacks for pending requests are not being invoked.

Let me trace through the stream cleanup one more time, focusing on the defer block:

```go
defer func() {
    ...
    if hasReq {
        // There's an unfinished request, cancel it, otherwise it will be blocked forever.
        currentReq.callback(tsoRequestResult{}, currentReq.reqKeyspaceGroupID, finishWithErr)
    }
    
    ...
    
    close(s.pendingRequests)
    
    // Cancel remaining pending requests.
    for req := range s.pendingRequests {
        req.callback(tsoRequestResult{}, req.reqKeyspaceGroupID, errors.WithStack(finishWithErr))
    }
    
    ...
}()
```

This should call the callback for all pending requests. But wait! What if there's a race condition where a request is being added to `pendingRequests` WHILE the defer block is executing?

Looking at line 295-305 of stream.go:

```go
select {
case s.pendingRequests <- batchedRequests{...}:
default:
    s.state.Store(prevState)
    return errors.New("unexpected channel full")
}
```

And then at line 361:

```go
close(s.pendingRequests)
```

AH! There's the race condition! If:
1. The recvLoop exits and starts executing the defer block
2. It reaches line 361 and closes `s.pendingRequests`
3. But BEFORE that, another goroutine is inside `processRequests` and is trying to send to `s.pendingRequests` (line 296)
4. If the send happens AFTER the close, it will panic
5. If the send happens BEFORE the drain loop (line 364), it will be handled
6. BUT, if the state check at line 277 passes (returns `streamStateIdle`), and THEN the defer block changes the state to `streamStateClosing` and closes the channel, the send at line 296 might happen on a closed channel!

Wait, that's not right. The state is checked at line 277-293, and if the state is `streamStateClosing`, it returns an error. So the synchronization should work...

Let me look at the state synchronization more carefully. The defer block at line 346-357:

```go
for !s.state.CompareAndSwap(streamStateIdle, streamStateClosing) {
    switch state := s.state.Load(); state {
    case streamStateIdle, streamStateSending:
        // streamStateSending should switch to streamStateIdle very quickly. Spin until successfully setting to
        // streamStateClosing.
        continue
    case streamStateClosing:
        log.Warn("unexpected double closing of tsoStream", zap.String("stream", s.streamID))
    default:
        log.Fatal("unknown tsoStream state", zap.String("stream", s.streamID), zap.Int32("state", state))
    }
}
```

This spins until it can change the state from `streamStateIdle` to `streamStateClosing`. This should ensure that no new requests can be added after this point.

So the race condition should be handled... But wait! Let me check the timing more carefully:

Looking at `processRequests` in stream.go (lines 277-306):
1. Line 277: Swap state to `streamStateSending`
2. Line 295-301: Send to `pendingRequests` channel
3. Line 306: Restore state to `prevState` (which is `streamStateIdle`)
4. Line 308: Call `s.stream.Send()`

And in the defer block:
1. Line 346: Try to swap state from `streamStateIdle` to `streamStateClosing`
2. Line 361: Close `pendingRequests`
3. Line 364: Drain `pendingRequests`

The problem is that there's a window between line 296 (when the request is added to `pendingRequests`) and line 308 (when `s.stream.Send()` is called). If the stream's Recv() encounters an error during this window:
1. The request is in `pendingRequests`
2. But `s.stream.Send()` hasn't been called yet or fails
3. The recvLoop tries to receive but gets an error
4. The recvLoop's defer block drains `pendingRequests` and calls the callbacks

This should still work correctly! The callback will be called with an error.

Hmm, let me think about this differently. Let me look at what happens to the `done` channel that's passed to `processRequests`:

Looking at line 326 of dispatcher.go:

```go

```

And then the callback at line 439:

```go
close(done)
```

The `done` channel is closed when the callback is invoked. The deadline watcher is waiting for this channel to be closed. If the callback is never invoked, the deadline watcher goroutine will wait forever (or until timeout).

So the goroutine leak is in the deadline watcher! If the callback is not invoked for some reason, the deadline watcher goroutine will leak!

Now, when is the callback NOT invoked? Let me trace through all the code paths...

The callback is created in dispatcher.go at line 436, and passed to `stream.processRequests` at line 463. The stream stores it in the `batchedRequests` struct at line 296-300 of stream.go. Then:

1. If `Recv()` succeeds, the callback is invoked at line 444 of stream.go
2. If `Recv()` fails, the recvLoop breaks and:
   - If `hasReq` is true (a request was popped from `pendingRequests`), the callback is invoked at line 341
   - All remaining requests in `pendingRequests` have their callbacks invoked at line 365

So the callback SHOULD always be invoked...

The comment says "the request should finally be canceled by the recvLoop". But what if the recvLoop is BLOCKED on `Recv()` and the Send error doesn't immediately cause a Recv error?

In gRPC, if Send fails, it doesn't necessarily mean Recv will immediately return an error. The Recv might continue to block if there's no error on the receiving side yet.

So here's the problematic scenario:
1. `processRequests` is called, request is added to `pendingRequests` (line 296)
2. `s.stream.Send()` is called and FAILS with an error (line 308)
3. The function returns `nil`, making the dispatcher think everything is fine (line 316)
4. The recvLoop is BLOCKED on `s.stream.Recv()` (line 403) and hasn't detected the error yet
5. The dispatcher continues and eventually exits or detects the error via `GetRecvError()`
6. The dispatcher cancels the stream's context, which causes `Recv()` to return with a context.Canceled error
7. The recvLoop's defer block runs and drains `pendingRequests`, calling all callbacks
8. Everything should be cleaned up...

But wait, there's still a timing issue. What if the dispatcher exits BEFORE the stream's cleanup completes? Let me check if the dispatcher waits for stream cleanup...

```go
defer func() {
    log.Info("[tso] exit tso dispatcher")
    // Cancel all connections.
    conCtxMgr.ReleaseAll()
    if tsoBatchController != nil && tsoBatchController.GetCollectedRequestCount() != 0 {
        // If you encounter this failure, please check the stack in the logs to see if it's a panic.
        log.Fatal("batched tso requests not cleared when exiting the tso dispatcher loop", zap.Any("panic", recover()))
    }
    tsoErr := errors.WithStack(errs.ErrClosing)
    td.revokePendingRequests(tsoErr)
    wg.Done()
}()
```

The dispatcher calls `conCtxMgr.ReleaseAll()` which cancels all streams. But it doesn't wait for the streams to finish cleanup! This means:
1. The dispatcher exits
2. The stream's cleanup is still running asynchronously
3. The deadline watcher goroutines are still waiting for callbacks
4. Eventually the cleanup completes and callbacks are invoked
5. The deadline watcher goroutines exit

So there shouldn't be a permanent leak, just a delay in cleanup...

```go
    cc, ok := c.connectionCtxs[url]
    if !ok {
        return
    }
    cc.Cancel()
    delete(c.connectionCtxs, url)
}
```

This calls `cc.Cancel()` which should cancel the context. But what if there's a case where the context is not properly set up?

The stream creates its OWN context and cancel function! This is a child context of the one passed in. So when the connection manager's cancel is called, it cancels the parent context, which should also cancel the child context.

Actually, I think the REAL issue is even more subtle. Let me look at what happens when the Send fails:


The function returns `nil` when Send fails! This is intentional because the request has already been added to `pendingRequests`, and the assumption is that the recvLoop will handle it.

1. The request is in `pendingRequests`
2. `processRequests` returns `nil` (success)

1. A timeout occurs
2. The context is cancelled
3. An actual receive error occurs

This is the correct behavior! When Send fails, it should return an error. But it's commented out with the explanation that "the request should finally be canceled by the recvLoop".

The problem with this logic is that it assumes the recvLoop will quickly detect the error and clean up. But if the recvLoop is blocked on Recv, it might take a long time (or forever) to detect the error.

So the fix is: When `Send()` fails, return an error immediately instead of relying on the recvLoop to clean up!

So we need to be careful about double-cleanup. Let me think about the correct fix...

Actually, I think the real solution is: When `Send()` fails, we should NOT return nil. We should either:
1. Remove the request from `pendingRequests` before returning an error
2. OR, immediately cancel the stream's context to force the recvLoop to exit quickly

Option 2 seems better because it's simpler and doesn't require removing from the channel. But we need to make sure the stream's cleanup happens quickly.

Actually, looking at the code again, I think the issue is that when Send fails, we're NOT cancelling the stream! We're just returning nil and hoping the recvLoop will eventually detect the error. But if the Recv is blocked, it might never detect the error (unless the context is cancelled).

So the fix is: When Send fails, immediately cancel the stream to ensure the recvLoop exits and cleans up the pending request!

+package main
+
+import (
+"context"
+"fmt"
+"runtime"
+"sync"
+"time"
+
+"github.com/tikv/pd/client/clients/tso"
+"github.com/tikv/pd/client/opt"
+cctx "github.com/tikv/pd/client/pkg/connectionctx"
+sd "github.com/tikv/pd/client/servicediscovery"
+)
+
+// Mock implementation for testing
+type mockTSOServiceProvider struct {
+option       *opt.Option
+createStream func(ctx context.Context) *tso.tsoStream
+conCtxMgr    *cctx.Manager[*tso.tsoStream]
+}
+
+func newMockTSOServiceProvider(option *opt.Option, createStream func(ctx context.Context) *tso.tsoStream) *mockTSOServiceProvider {
+return &mockTSOServiceProvider{
+option:       option,
+createStream: createStream,
+conCtxMgr:    cctx.NewManager[*tso.tsoStream](),
+}
+}
+
+func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getOption() *opt.Option {
+return m.option
+}
+
+func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getServiceDiscovery() sd.ServiceDiscovery {
+return sd.NewMockServiceDiscovery([]string{"mock:///"}, nil)
+}
+
+func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) getConnectionCtxMgr() *cctx.Manager[*tso.tsoStream] {
+return m.conCtxMgr
+}
+
+func (m *mockTSOServiceProvider) updateConnectionCtxs(ctx context.Context) bool {
+if m.conCtxMgr.Exist("mock:///") {
+return true
+}
+cctx, cancel := context.WithCancel(ctx)
+var stream *tso.tsoStream
+if m.createStream != nil {
+stream = m.createStream(ctx)
+}
+if stream != nil {
+m.conCtxMgr.Store(cctx, cancel, "mock:///", stream)
+return true
+}
+cancel()
+return false
+}
+
+func main() {
+fmt.Println("Testing goroutine leak scenario...")
+
+// Get initial goroutine count
+runtime.GC()
+time.Sleep(100 * time.Millisecond)
+initialGoroutines := runtime.NumGoroutine()
+fmt.Printf("Initial goroutines: %d\n", initialGoroutines)
+
+option := opt.NewOption()
+option.Timeout = time.Hour
+
+ctx := context.Background()
+
+// We'll test if goroutines leak when stream errors occur
+// Create a provider that will help us simulate stream errors
+provider := newMockTSOServiceProvider(option, nil)
+
+// Note: This is a simplified test - the actual leak reproduction requires
+// access to internal tsoDispatcher methods and stream creation
+fmt.Println("Test setup complete")
+
+// Check goroutine count
+runtime.GC()
+time.Sleep(100 * time.Millisecond)
+finalGoroutines := runtime.NumGoroutine()
+fmt.Printf("Final goroutines: %d\n", finalGoroutines)
+
+if finalGoroutines > initialGoroutines+2 {
+fmt.Printf("WARNING: Potential goroutine leak detected! Leaked: %d\n", finalGoroutines-initialGoroutines)
+} else {
+fmt.Println("No significant goroutine leak detected in this simplified test")
+}
+}
